Al-Nazzam’s Refutation of Manichaean Dualism
Al-Nazzam refutes Manichaean dualism through a rhetorical question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's logical argument that the mixture of light and darkness requires an external unifier, refuting the Manichaean claim of self-originating dualism.

Core Idea
Opposites cannot unite by themselves; their union proves a unifier.

Al-Nazzam confronts the Manichaean worldview that posits two eternal principles—light and darkness—whose mixture produced the world. He uses a simple but devastating logical question: if these two are truly opposite and independent, how could they ever come together on their own? The argument exposes the need for a third, subjugating agent, which for him is none other than God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage aloud if you can, then the translation. Notice the rhetorical question form—al-Nazzam is not stating a fact but challenging his opponents.
The passage is short but dense. Read it slowly.
2
Identify the Manichaean claim
The Manichaeans held that light and darkness are eternal, opposite principles, and that the world resulted from their mixture. Understand that claim before following the refutation.
3
Follow the logic of the question
Al-Nazzam asks: if they were as you described (opposite and independent), how could they mix, interpenetrate, and unite of their own accord? The implied answer: they cannot. They would only diverge.
He uses 'of their own accord' to emphasize the lack of an external cause.
4
Recognize the unifier implied
The argument concludes that mixture must be caused by a subduer (qāhir) and a unifier (jāmi‘). This points to the existence of a single Creator.
This is a classic kalam argument from the impossibility of self-caused combination of opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا كانا على ما وصفتم فكيف امتزجا وتداخلا واجتمعا من تلقاء أنفسهما ، وليس يمنع فوقها قاهر قهرهما ولا جامع جمعهما ومنعها من أعمنا لما كما الخير فما في طبعة من الاشدار وكما يمنع الماء مما. الماء ثما في طبعه من السيلان بل ينبغي أن يكونا لا يزدادان إلا تبايناً ومفارقة على قولكم ؟
If they were as you described, how did they mix, interpenetrate, and unite of their own accord, with no subjugator above them subjugating them and no unifier uniting them and preventing them from [?] as the water prevents [?] in its nature of flowing? Rather, they should not increase except in divergence and separation, according to your statement?
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Manichaean claim that the world originated from the mixture of light and darkness. Al-Nazzam argues that if light and darkness were truly opposite and independent, they would never unite by themselves; their mixture requires a subduer. The rhetorical question shows the impossibility of self-mixture.

Key Terms
مانوية
نور
ظلمة
امتزاج
قاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Nazzam is denying that light and darkness can mix at all, but he is only denying that they can mix without a cause.
Correction: Al-Nazzam's argument is about the need for an external agent, not about the impossibility of mixture per se.

Checkpoint
What is al-Nazzam's main argument against the Manichaeans?
A) Light and darkness cannot mix under any circumstances.
B) Light and darkness must have a unifier because opposites cannot unite by themselves.
C) Darkness is stronger than light.
D) The world was created from nothing.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's rhetorical question exposes the fatal flaw in dualism: opposites cannot unite themselves, so their union points to a single Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where good and bad are mixed in your life, do you ever unconsciously assume that two opposing forces are at work? How does Al-Nazzam's argument challenge you to recognize that only Allah can unite opposites—and that He is in control of both the light and darkness you experience? 

Think of one area where you have felt a 'battle' between good and bad. Will you consciously affirm that Allah alone is the unifier of all that happens, and ask Him to help you see His hand in that mixture today?
?
The Manichaeans believed darkness (evil) is an eternal principle independent of light. Is there any part of your life where you treat your own sins or trials as if they have independent power over you? 

Based on this lesson, what truth must you hold onto when you feel overwhelmed by evil—whether inside or outside you? Write one concrete step you will take to submit that struggle back to the One who alone can subjugate it.
?
Al-Nazzam forces the dualist to answer: 'How did they mix of their own accord?' 

Now consider your own heart: do you experience a conflict between your desire to obey Allah and your inclination to sin? Who is the unifier that can bring your scattered intentions together? 

What is one specific intention you can surrender to Him today, trusting that He alone can make your will and His command meet?

Maturidi's Refutation of Materialists and Dualists
The analyst lists the sects Maturidi refuted in Kitab al-Tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will identify the non-Muslim sects Maturidi addressed in Kitab al-Tawhid and understand the scope of his refutations.

Core Idea
Maturidi systematically refutes materialists and three dualist subgroups.

This passage begins by noting that Maturidi devoted specific chapters to presenting and refuting the views of Magians, heretics, materialists, and dualists. It then names three distinct dualist groups—Manichaeans, Daysanites, and Marcionites—along with the naturalists among the materialists, showing the breadth of his theological engagement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation.
Notice the terms for each group.
2
List the sects mentioned
Identify each group: Magians, heretics, materialists, dualists, naturalists, Manichaeans, Daysanites, Marcionites.
These are the main targets of Maturidi's refutation.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Maturidi would choose to address these specific groups. How does this fit into his broader theological project?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفرد الماتريدي فصولا في كتاب التوحيد عرض فيها لآراء المجوس والزنادقة من دهرية وثنوية . ونقض أقوال أصحاب الطبائع من الدهريين ، كما نقض أقوال الثنوية من مانوية وديصانية ومرقيونية ، ويعتبر كتاب التوحيد من أقدم الوثائق الهامة التي بين أيدينا والتي نجد فيها عرضا موسعاً لآراء أصحاب هذه المذاهب أقدم من فهرست ابن النديم المتوفى عام ٣٧٩ هـ / ۹۸۷ م وأقدم من كتاب المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار المتوفى عام ٤١٥ هـ / ١٠٢٤ م . وأقدم من الملل والنحل للشهرستاني المتوفى عام ٥٥٨ هـ / ١١٥٣ م . الصعب أن نحدد مقدار ما أسهم به الماتريدي من نقض لآراء هذه الفرق ؛ لأننا نعلم أن المعتزلة الذين فقدت معظم مؤلفاتهم ، وخاصة مؤلفات شيوخهم الأوائل - كانوا من أسبق المتكلمين الذين قاموا بدور كبير في هذا الشأن وقطعوا في مناظراتهم كثيراً من أرباب هذه المذاهب . وإذا عددنا الجزء الخاص بالفرق غير الاسلامية من كتاب المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار الذي جاء بعد الماتريدي حصيلة آراء المعتزلة في مذاهب هذه الفرق ومناقشتها فإننا نجد بين كتاب المغني وكتاب التوحيد شبهاً كبيرا . فمن ناحية نجد صاحب المغني يعتمد صراحة في حكايته لمذهب المانوية على عيسى الوراق المتوفى عام ٢٤٧ / ٨٦١ م ومن كتبه في مذاهب الثنوية : فنقرأ في الفصل الذي كتبه بعنوان 'حكاية قبل المانوية' : وحكى أبو عيسى الوراق عن أكثرهم أن النور لم يزل مرتفعاً من ناحية الشمال . والظلمة منحطة من ناحية الجنوب ونقرأ في موضع آخر : وحكي عن أبي عيسى الوراق أن أفعال ، وجاء في موضع آخر : ' وقال الوراق في كتابه ، وكان ثنوياً : هم على ثلاث فرق . فرقة تنفي الأعراض ، وأخرى تثبتها أغياراً للأجسام'.
And Maturidi devoted chapters in Kitab al-Tawhid wherein he presented the views of the Magians and the heretics (zanadiqa) among the materialists (dahriyya) and dualists (thanawiyya). And he refuted the statements of the naturalists (ashab al-taba'i') from among the materialists, just as he refuted the statements of the dualists: the Manichaeans (Manawiyya), the Daysanites (Daysaniyya), and the Marcionites (Marqiyuniyya). And Kitab al-Tawhid is considered one of the oldest important documents in our possession in which we find an extensive presentation of the views of the adherents of these sects, older than the Fihrist of Ibn al-Nadim (d. 379 AH/987 CE), and older than the Mughni of Qadi Abd al-Jabbar (d. 415 AH/1024 CE), and older than the Milal wa al-Nihal of al-Shahrastani (d. 558 AH/1153 CE). It is difficult to determine the extent of Maturidi's contribution in refuting the views of these sects, because we know that the Mu'tazila, most of whose works have been lost, especially the works of their early masters, were among the earliest theologians who played a great role in this matter and cut off many of the adherents of these sects in their debates. And if we consider the section on non-Islamic sects from the Mughni of Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, which came after Maturidi as a product of Mu'tazili views on the sects and their discussion, we find great similarity between the Mughni and Kitab al-Tawhid. On one hand, we find that the author of the Mughni explicitly relies in his account of the Manichaean doctrine on Isa al-Warraq (d. 247 AH/861 CE) and his books on the doctrines of the dualists. In the chapter he wrote entitled 'Account of the Manichaeans', he said: 'Abu Isa al-Warraq reported from most of them that light has never ceased being elevated from the direction of the north, and darkness descended from the direction of the south.' And in another place: 'It was related from Abu Isa al-Warraq that actions...' And in another place: 'Al-Warraq said in his book—and he was a dualist—: They are three sects: a sect that denies accidents (a'rad), and another that affirms them as modes (aghayar) of bodies.'
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage, written by a modern analyst, highlights the importance of Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid as an early source for the study of non-Islamic sects, particularly dualists and materialists. It first lists the sects Maturidi engaged with: Magians, heretics, materialists, and dualist groups like Manichaeans, Daysanites, and Marcionites. It then asserts the book's chronological priority over later classical works like Ibn al-Nadim's Fihrist, Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's Mughni, and Shahrastani's Milal wa al-Nihal. The passage acknowledges the difficulty of measuring Maturidi's original contribution because of the loss of early Mu'tazili works. However, it points to a significant parallel: the Mughni's author relied on the same source, Isa al-Warraq, for information on Manichaeism, and the similarities between the two works suggest a common source tradition. The passage ends with a sample quote from al-Warraq about Manichaean cosmology and dualist classification of sects.

Key Terms
dahriyya
thanawiyya
Manawiyya
Daysaniyya
Marqiyuniyya
ashab al-taba'i'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following dualist groups is NOT mentioned in the passage?
A) Manichaeans
B) Daysanites
C) Marcionites
D) Zoroastrians

Key Takeaway
Maturidi devoted specific chapters to refuting materialists, naturalists, and three dualist sects in Kitab al-Tawhid.

Kitab al-Tawhid's Place in Islamic Heresiography
The analyst discusses the chronological priority of Kitab al-Tawhid and its relationship to later works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the historical significance of Kitab al-Tawhid as an early source for non-Muslim sects and its connection to later heresiographical works.

Core Idea
Kitab al-Tawhid is older than major heresiographies and shares sources with the Mu'tazili tradition.

The passage continues by asserting that Kitab al-Tawhid is one of the oldest surviving documents containing extensive information on these sects, predating Ibn al-Nadim's Fihrist, Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's Mughni, and al-Shahrastani's Milal wa al-Nihal. It then discusses the difficulty of assessing Maturidi's originality due to lost Mu'tazili works and highlights the common source tradition through Isa al-Warraq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic from 'ويعتبر كتاب التوحيد' to the end. Note the chronological comparisons.
2
Identify the earlier books
List the three works that are younger than Kitab al-Tawhid: Fihrist, Mughni, Milal wa al-Nihal.
This shows the early date of Maturidi's work.
3
Understand the source problem
Reflect on why it is hard to measure Maturidi's contribution: lost Mu'tazili works. Then see the similarity with the Mughni and the reliance on Isa al-Warraq.
This points to a shared textual tradition.

وأفرد الماتريدي فصولا في كتاب التوحيد عرض فيها لآراء المجوس والزنادقة من دهرية وثنوية . ونقض أقوال أصحاب الطبائع من الدهريين ، كما نقض أقوال الثنوية من مانوية وديصانية ومرقيونية ، ويعتبر كتاب التوحيد من أقدم الوثائق الهامة التي بين أيدينا والتي نجد فيها عرضا موسعاً لآراء أصحاب هذه المذاهب أقدم من فهرست ابن النديم المتوفى عام ٣٧٩ هـ / ۹۸۷ م وأقدم من كتاب المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار المتوفى عام ٤١٥ هـ / ١٠٢٤ م . وأقدم من الملل والنحل للشهرستاني المتوفى عام ٥٥٨ هـ / ١١٥٣ م . الصعب أن نحدد مقدار ما أسهم به الماتريدي من نقض لآراء هذه الفرق ؛ لأننا نعلم أن المعتزلة الذين فقدت معظم مؤلفاتهم ، وخاصة مؤلفات شيوخهم الأوائل - كانوا من أسبق المتكلمين الذين قاموا بدور كبير في هذا الشأن وقطعوا في مناظراتهم كثيراً من أرباب هذه المذاهب . وإذا عددنا الجزء الخاص بالفرق غير الاسلامية من كتاب المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار الذي جاء بعد الماتريدي حصيلة آراء المعتزلة في مذاهب هذه الفرق ومناقشتها فإننا نجد بين كتاب المغني وكتاب التوحيد شبهاً كبيرا . فمن ناحية نجد صاحب المغني يعتمد صراحة في حكايته لمذهب المانوية على عيسى الوراق المتوفى عام ٢٤٧ / ٨٦١ م ومن كتبه في مذاهب الثنوية : فنقرأ في الفصل الذي كتبه بعنوان 'حكاية قبل المانوية' : وحكى أبو عيسى الوراق عن أكثرهم أن النور لم يزل مرتفعاً من ناحية الشمال . والظلمة منحطة من ناحية الجنوب ونقرأ في موضع آخر : وحكي عن أبي عيسى الوراق أن أفعال ، وجاء في موضع آخر : ' وقال الوراق في كتابه ، وكان ثنوياً : هم على ثلاث فرق . فرقة تنفي الأعراض ، وأخرى تثبتها أغياراً للأجسام'.
And Maturidi devoted chapters in Kitab al-Tawhid wherein he presented the views of the Magians and the heretics (zanadiqa) among the materialists (dahriyya) and dualists (thanawiyya). And he refuted the statements of the naturalists (ashab al-taba'i') from among the materialists, just as he refuted the statements of the dualists: the Manichaeans (Manawiyya), the Daysanites (Daysaniyya), and the Marcionites (Marqiyuniyya). And Kitab al-Tawhid is considered one of the oldest important documents in our possession in which we find an extensive presentation of the views of the adherents of these sects, older than the Fihrist of Ibn al-Nadim (d. 379 AH/987 CE), and older than the Mughni of Qadi Abd al-Jabbar (d. 415 AH/1024 CE), and older than the Milal wa al-Nihal of al-Shahrastani (d. 558 AH/1153 CE). It is difficult to determine the extent of Maturidi's contribution in refuting the views of these sects, because we know that the Mu'tazila, most of whose works have been lost, especially the works of their early masters, were among the earliest theologians who played a great role in this matter and cut off many of the adherents of these sects in their debates. And if we consider the section on non-Islamic sects from the Mughni of Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, which came after Maturidi as a product of Mu'tazili views on the sects and their discussion, we find great similarity between the Mughni and Kitab al-Tawhid. On one hand, we find that the author of the Mughni explicitly relies in his account of the Manichaean doctrine on Isa al-Warraq (d. 247 AH/861 CE) and his books on the doctrines of the dualists. In the chapter he wrote entitled 'Account of the Manichaeans', he said: 'Abu Isa al-Warraq reported from most of them that light has never ceased being elevated from the direction of the north, and darkness descended from the direction of the south.' And in another place: 'It was related from Abu Isa al-Warraq that actions...' And in another place: 'Al-Warraq said in his book—and he was a dualist—: They are three sects: a sect that denies accidents (a'rad), and another that affirms them as modes (aghayar) of bodies.'
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage, written by a modern analyst, highlights the importance of Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid as an early source for the study of non-Islamic sects, particularly dualists and materialists. It first lists the sects Maturidi engaged with: Magians, heretics, materialists, and dualist groups like Manichaeans, Daysanites, and Marcionites. It then asserts the book's chronological priority over later classical works like Ibn al-Nadim's Fihrist, Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's Mughni, and Shahrastani's Milal wa al-Nihal. The passage acknowledges the difficulty of measuring Maturidi's original contribution because of the loss of early Mu'tazili works. However, it points to a significant parallel: the Mughni's author relied on the same source, Isa al-Warraq, for information on Manichaeism, and the similarities between the two works suggest a common source tradition. The passage ends with a sample quote from al-Warraq about Manichaean cosmology and dualist classification of sects.

dahriyya
thanawiyya
Manawiyya
Isa al-Warraq

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's work is entirely original and independent of earlier sources.
Correction: The passage shows that Kitab al-Tawhid shares similarities with later Mu'tazili works and likely relies on Isa al-Warraq, indicating a common source tradition.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is one of the oldest documents for the views of dualist and materialist sects?
A) Fihrist of Ibn al-Nadim
B) Mughni of Qadi Abd al-Jabbar
C) Milal wa al-Nihal
D) Kitab al-Tawhid

Key Takeaway
Kitab al-Tawhid is an early source for non-Muslim sects, predating major heresiographies, and shares textual traditions with later Mu'tazili works.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This passage shows that even great scholars like Maturidi depended on earlier sources. How can you develop the same critical eye in your own study of religion? Will you commit to checking the provenance of your next piece of religious knowledge?
?
The passage notes that many Mu'tazili works are lost. This should remind you that our knowledge of Islamic intellectual history is incomplete. Does this realization make you more eager to seek knowledge from primary sources, or do you rely too much on summaries? What specific step will you take this week to consult a primary text?
?
Maturidi used a dualist writer as a source to understand other religions. Are you willing to learn about other faiths from their own sources, or do you only read refutations? Could this humility in learning from others, even those you disagree with, be a model for how you approach those with different beliefs today?

Maturidi's Use of Sources in Refuting Dualists
Analysis of Maturidi's sources for dualist refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid relies on earlier sources, particularly Abu 'Isa al-Warraq, for his refutation of dualist sects, and how his account compares with al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's al-Mughni.

Core Idea
Maturidi and his contemporary sources shared a common well for refuting dualists.

The passage shows that Maturidi's work is a key early document, but his refutations likely build on earlier Mu'tazilite and dualist sources. By comparing Kitab al-Tawhid with al-Mughni, scholars find striking similarities, suggesting a shared origin in the writings of Abu 'Isa al-Warraq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the scholarly analysis. Note that it discusses Maturidi's book as an early source and compares it with later works.
2
Identify Maturidi's contribution
Observe that Kitab al-Tawhid is one of the oldest documents presenting dualist views, predating Ibn al-Nadim, al-Mughni, and al-Shahrastani.
3
Understand the comparative method
The author compares the non-Islamic sects section of al-Mughni with Kitab al-Tawhid and finds great similarity, suggesting a common source.
4
Trace the reliance on al-Warraq
Notice that al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar explicitly cites Abu 'Isa al-Warraq, and Maturidi also agrees with his transmission. Both likely drew from al-Warraq's works or a shared earlier source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفرد الماتريدي فصولا في كتاب التوحيد عرض فيها لآراء المجوس والزنادقة من دهرية وثنوية. ونقض أقوال أصحاب الطبائع من الدهريين، كما نقض أقوال الثنوية من مانوية وديصانية ومرقيونية، ويعتبر كتاب التوحيد من أقدم الوثائق الهامة التي بين أيدينا والتي نجد فيها عرضا موسعاً لآراء أصحاب هذه المذاهب أقدم من فهرست ابن النديم المتوفى عام ٣٧٩ هـ / ٩٨٧ م وأقدم من كتاب المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار المتوفى عام ٤١٥ هـ / ١٠٢٤ م. وأقدم من الملل والنحل للشهرستاني المتوفى عام ٥٥٨ هـ / ١١٥٣ م. من الصعب أن نحدد مقدار ما أسهم به الماتريدي من نقض لآراء هذه الفرق؛ لأننا نعلم أن المعتزلة الذين فقدت معظم مؤلفاتهم، وخاصة مؤلفات شيوخهم الأوائل - كانوا من أسبق المتكلمين الذين قاموا بدور كبير في هذا الشأن وقطعوا في مناظراتهم كثيراً من أرباب هذه المذاهب. وإذا عددنا الجزء الخاص بالفرق غير الاسلامية من كتاب المغني للقاضي عبد الجبار الذي جاء بعد الماتريدي حصيلة آراء المعتزلة في مذاهب هذه الفرق ومناقشتها فإننا نجد بين كتاب المغني وكتاب التوحيد شبهاً كبيرا. فمن ناحية نجد صاحب المغني يعتمد صراحة في حكايته لمذهب المانوية على عيسى الوراق المتوفى عام ٥٢٤٧ / ٨٦١ م و من كتبه في مذاهب الثنوية: فنقرأ في الفصل الذي كتبه بعنوان "حكاية قبل المانوية؟ :: وحكى أبو أن النور لم يزل مرتفعاً من ناحية الشمال. والظلمة منحطة من ناحية الجنوب ونقرأ في موضع آخر: وحكي عن أبي عيسى الوراق أن أفعال، وجاء في موضع آخر: " وقال الوراق في كتابه، وكان ثنوياً: هم على ثلاث فرق. فرقة تنفي الأعراض، وأخرى تثبتها أغياراً للأجسام، وثالثة زعمت أنها صفات، ولا يقال هي الجسم أو غيره، وكذلك يفعل الماتريدي؛ إذ بين الأستاذ جورج قيدا في مقالته عن الماتريدي وحكايته لمذاهب المانوية والديصانية والمرقيونية أن الماتريدي يتفق مع أبي عيسى الوراق في روايته لهذه المذاهب. وعلى ذلك الاستاذ فيدا أن يكون الوراق والماتريدي قد استقيا معلوماتهما عن هذه الفرق من منبع واحد. هذا فضلاً عن أن الماتريدي يذكر الوراق صراحة في أكثر من موضع في كتاب التوحيد. ومن ناحية أخرى نجد اتفاقاً بين القاضي عبد الجبار والماتريدي في روايتهما لمذاهب الثنوية. فنقرأ في كتاب المغني في الفصل الذي عقده القاضي عبد الجبار في حكاية قول المانوية: وزعموا أن كل واحد منهما [أي النور والظلمة] خمسة أجناس ... وحكي أن الأجناس الخمسة منها سواد وبياض وحمرة وصفرة وخضرة، فما كان منها من بياض في عالم النور فهو خير، وما كان منه في عالم الظلمة فهو شر. ولنا حواس فما كان منها في النور فهو خير، وما كان منها في الظلمة فهو شر. ونقرأ في كتاب التوحيد في الفصل الذي ذكر فيه الماتريدي أقاويل المنانية: ولكل واحد منها أي النور والظلمة خمسة أجناس: حمرة وبياض وصفرة وسواد وخضرة، فلكل شيء مما جاء من هذا الجنس من جوهر النور فهو خير، وما الظلمة فهر شر. وكذلك لكل واحد منها حواس خمس ... فما كان من جوهر أدرك جوهر النور بها فهو خير، وما أدرك جوهر الظلمة فهو شر.
And Maturidi devoted chapters in Kitab al-Tawhid in which he presented the opinions of the Magians and the zindiqs, both materialists (Dahriyya) and dualists (Thanawiyya). And he refuted the statements of the proponents of natures (taba’i‘) from the materialists, as he refuted the statements of the dualists from the Manichaeans, Dayzanites, and Marcionites. Kitab al-Tawhid is considered among the oldest important documents in our hands in which we find an extensive presentation of the opinions of the adherents of these sects, older than the Fihrist of Ibn al-Nadim (d. 379 AH / 987 CE) and older than the Mughni of al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar (d. 415 AH / 1024 CE), and older than al-Milal wa al-Nihal of al-Shahrastani (d. 558 AH / 1153 CE). It is difficult to determine the extent of Maturidi's contribution in refuting the opinions of these sects; because we know that the Mu'tazilites, whose most works are lost, especially the works of their early masters, were among the earliest mutakallimun who played a great role in this matter and cut off many of the adherents of these sects in their debates. And if we consider the section on non-Islamic sects from the Mughni of al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, which came after Maturidi, as the sum of Mu'tazilite opinions on the doctrines of these sects and their discussion, we find great similarity between the Mughni and Kitab al-Tawhid. On one hand, we find that the author of the Mughni explicitly relies in his narration of the Manichaean doctrine on 'Isa al-Warraq (d. 247 AH / 861 CE) and his books on the doctrines of the dualists: we read in the chapter he wrote entitled 'Narration before the Manichaeans? :: And Abu narrated that light has never ceased rising from the direction of the north, and darkness descending from the direction of the south,' and we read in another place: 'And it is narrated from Abu 'Isa al-Warraq that actions,' and in another place: 'And al-Warraq said in his book, and he was a dualist: They are of three sects: a sect that denies accidents, another that affirms them as other than bodies, and a third that claimed they are attributes, and it is not said that they are the body or something else.' And likewise does Maturidi; for Professor George Vajda showed in his article on Maturidi and his narration of the doctrines of the Manichaeans, Dayzanites, and Marcionites that Maturidi agrees with Abu 'Isa al-Warraq in his transmission of these doctrines. On that basis, Professor Vajda concluded that al-Warraq and Maturidi must have drawn their information about these sects from a single source. This is in addition to the fact that Maturidi explicitly mentions al-Warraq in more than one place in Kitab al-Tawhid. On the other hand, we find agreement between al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar and Maturidi in their narration of the doctrines of the dualists. We read in the Mughni in the chapter that al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar devoted to narrating the statement of the Manichaeans: 'And they claimed that each one of them [i.e., light and darkness] [has] five kinds ... and it is narrated that the five kinds include black, white, red, yellow, and green; what is of them from white in the world of light is good, and what is of them in the world of darkness is evil. And we have senses; what is of them in light is good, and what is of them in darkness is evil.' And we read in Kitab al-Tawhid in the chapter in which Maturidi mentioned the sayings of the Manichaeans: 'And each one of them, i.e., light and darkness, has five kinds: red, white, yellow, black, and green; for everything that comes from this kind from the substance of light is good, and what [comes] from darkness is evil. And likewise each one of them has five senses ... so what is from the substance that perceives the substance of light by them is good, and what perceives the substance of darkness is evil.'
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage is a scholarly analysis of Maturidi's methodology in Kitab al-Tawhid regarding his refutation of non-Islamic sects, specifically dualists (Thanawiyya) like Manichaeans, Dayzanites, and Marcionites, as well as materialists (Dahriyya). It begins by noting that Maturidi dedicated chapters to presenting and refuting the views of these groups. The author then highlights the historical value of Kitab al-Tawhid as one of the oldest surviving documents that provide an extensive account of these sects, predating Ibn al-Nadim's Fihrist, al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's al-Mughni, and al-Shahrastani's al-Milal wa al-Nihal. Next, the passage acknowledges the difficulty in assessing Maturidi's original contribution because the Mu'tazilites, whose early works are lost, had already engaged in refuting these sects. However, by comparing the non-Muslim sects section of al-Mughni with Kitab al-Tawhid, the author finds great similarity. The analysis reveals that al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar relied heavily on Abu 'Isa al-Warraq, a dualist author, for his account of Manichaeism. Quotations from al-Mughni show al-Warraq's narration of Manichaean cosmology (light from north, darkness from south) and a classification of dualist views on accidents. Maturidi likewise agrees with al-Warraq's transmission, as demonstrated by scholar George Vajda, suggesting both drew from a common source. Furthermore, Maturidi explicitly mentions al-Warraq in Kitab al-Tawhid. Finally, the passage illustrates agreement between al-Mughni and Kitab al-Tawhid on the specifics of Manichaean doctrine: both describe the five kinds (black, white, red, yellow, green) assigned to light and darkness, with good and evil determined by which substance they belong to, and similarly for the five senses. This textual comparison shows that Maturidi and Abd al-Jabbar likely used a shared source, possibly al-Warraq's works.

Correction: The passage shows that Maturidi likely depended on earlier sources, particularly Abu 'Isa al-Warraq, and his account aligns closely with al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's al-Mughni, suggesting a common source.

A) They are completely independent and differ significantly.
B) Al-Mughni is a direct copy of Kitab al-Tawhid.
C) They show great similarity, likely due to a common source.
D) There is no relationship between the two works.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid and al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's al-Mughdi share a common source in Abu 'Isa al-Warraq for their accounts of dualist doctrines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a creed that differs from your own, do you take the time to understand it from its original sources, or do you rely on second-hand accounts that may be distorted? How does Maturidi's reliance on Abu 'Isa al-Warraq challenge you to be more diligent in your own research?
?
The passage shows that Maturidi and al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar both accurately quoted Manichaean doctrines from a shared source. Do you treat the beliefs of those you disagree with with such fairness, or do you misrepresent them to win an argument? What would it cost you to be as precise as these scholars were?
?
This lesson reveals that even the earliest Islamic theological works are built on earlier sources. When you read religious texts, do you assume the author's originality, or do you humbly recognize the chain of transmission? How will you change your approach to studying 'aqeedah after realizing this?

Refuting Mu'tazila on Permanence and Self-Subsistence
Maturidi criticizes Mu'tazila inconsistency on accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila's denial of permanence as an accident leads to a logical inconsistency that undermines tawhid.

Core Idea
Inconsistency on accidents can lead to denying God's role as the world's sustainer.

Maturidi continues his sharp critique of the Mu'tazila by pointing out a glaring contradiction in their view of accidents. They readily admit that motion and rest are accidents, yet they refuse to consider permanence and annihilation as accidents. This lesson unpacks his argument that such inconsistency opens the door to the world being self-subsistent, which would directly conflict with the principle of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila position
Read the passage and locate where Maturidi describes what the Mu'tazila accept and what they refuse regarding accidents.
They accept motion etc. as accidents but reject permanence and annihilation.
2
Understand the logical consequence
Maturidi argues that if permanence were not an accident, bodies would be self-subsistent. Consider why that would be problematic.
Self-subsistence implies no need for God's continuous preservation.
3
Connect to tawhid
Reflect on how this position spoils tawhid by denying that God alone is the self-subsistent sustainer.
The Mu'tazila also consider will and action as parts of the world, which compounds the error.
4
Apply the critique
Summarize in your own words why Maturidi says this position is more applicable to the Mu'tazila than others.
Their own theology makes God and the world too intertwined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثير من المعتزلة يقولون بهذا في الأول ، / ويأبون في البقاء والفناء ، مع ما يبطل قولهم ; إذ كان مختلفا بنفسه فى احتمال ذلك ، ولو جاز ذلك وجاز بقاء الأجسام بأنفسها الجاز كونها لا بغير . على أن هذا بالمعتزلة أولى ; لأنهم لا يجعلون الله إلى جملة العالم غير العالم ، به كان العالم ; إذ الإرادة يجعلونها من العالم والفعل كذلك ، وذاته كان موجودا عندهم ولم يكن العالم ولم يحدث شيء سوى كون العالم ، فإذا كان بنفسه وبه يبقى ، وفى ذلك فساد التوحيد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And many of the Mu'tazila affirm this regarding the first (i.e., motion etc.) but refuse regarding permanence and annihilation, despite what invalidates their statement; for (the body) differs in itself in bearing that. And if that were permissible and the permanence of bodies by themselves were permissible, it would be permissible for them to exist without another. Moreover, this is more applicable to the Mu'tazila, because they do not make God other than the entirety of the world; (they say) the world came to be by Him. For they make will (irada) part of the world, and action likewise. And to them His essence existed while the world was not, and nothing occurred except the coming to be of the world. So if it (the world) is by itself and by it (the accident) it remains, that would spoil tawhid. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila who accept that motion and rest are accidents but deny that permanence (baqa') and annihilation (fana') are accidents. Maturidi argues that if bodies could remain by themselves, they would not need God for their continuation, which leads to a denial of tawhid. Moreover, the Mu'tazila hold that God's will and action are within the world, not separate. If the world's permanence were intrinsic, it would not need God's ongoing preservation. This undermines tawhid because it suggests self-subsistence apart from God.

Key Terms
معتزلة
بقاء
فناء
إرادة
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that permanence is not an accident because it seems intrinsic to a thing.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if permanence were intrinsic, the world would not need God for its continued existence, which contradicts tawhid.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila deny according to Maturidi, and why does he say it undermines tawhid?
A) They deny that motion is an accident
B) They deny that permanence and annihilation are accidents; if permanence is intrinsic, then the world does not need God to continue
C) They deny that God exists
D) They deny that the world is created

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes that the Mu'tazila's inconsistency on accidents logically leads to a view where the world is self-subsistent, which directly violates tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself accepting God's role in the beginning of your existence but failing to recognize His constant maintenance of your life? How does this passage challenge that selective belief? What will you do to change that today?
?
Maturidi argues that inconsistency in theology leads to undermining tawhid. Where in your own belief about God's attributes do you hold inconsistent positions that might subtly diminish His unique role? Be honest — what is one attribute you struggle to consistently affirm?
?
If you truly believed that every moment of your existence is sustained directly by God (not by habit or nature), how would your daily habits change? What would you do differently right now to acknowledge that dependence?

Refutation of al-Ka'bi's Fallacy
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's inference that non-body implies accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's erroneous inference that anything not a body must be an accident.

Core Idea
Not being a body does not automatically make something an accident.

Maturidi here corrects a logical mistake by the Mu'tazili theologian al-Ka'bi. Al-Ka'bi claimed that if something is not a body, it must be an accident. Maturidi points out that this inference is flawed—accidents have their own defining properties. This brief passage establishes a fundamental distinction in Islamic theology between different categories of existents.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Read the passage and note al-Ka'bi's assertion that 'not being a body' implies 'being an accident'.
2
Understand Maturidi's correction
Why does Maturidi reject this? Because establishing something is not a body does not automatically make it an accident; accidents have specific characteristics.

Source Text

Likewise our statement in correcting the statement of al-Ka'bi: that when it is established that it is not a body, because it is an accident...
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage is a brief refutation of al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian. Maturidi states that it is a mistake to argue that something is an accident simply because it is not a body. The concise phrasing indicates that Maturidi rejects al-Ka'bi's inference. The full context would elaborate, but Maturidi asserts that 'not being a body' does not entail being an accident; accidents have their own definition.

Similarity is Based on Essence, Not on Names
Clarifying that similarity is based on essence, not names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why using the same name for God and creation does not imply similarity, and how similarity is determined by essences and actions, not by words.

Core Idea
Similarity is judged by essence and action, not by the name itself.

Imam Maturidi begins by clarifying a common misconception: that sharing a name with something necessitates similarity. He argues that true similarity is based on likeness between essences and actions, not on verbal labels. This foundational principle protects divine transcendence while allowing us to use the names revealed by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'shabah' (similarity) and 'dhāt' (essence).
2
Identify the core argument
Notice that the author states similarity returns to likeness between essences and actions, not to verbal labels. Highlight the phrase 'فإلى ذلك يرجع' (so to that it returns).
3
Consider the hypothetical about names
The author asks: if there were no names, how would things be known? This shows that names are not the basis of similarity.
This reinforces that essence and action determine likeness.
4
Apply the principle to God
Since God's essence is unique and not similar to any created being, using the same name does not create similarity. The author says 'لم يلزمنا التسمية بما تعرفنا' (the naming by what we know does not necessitate us).
This is a key takeaway for understanding divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما كان التشابه التى تقدره أوهامنا ليس عن قول اللسان نقدره، بل بما كنا نعرف الشبه بين الذاتين والفعلين، فإلى ذلك يرجع، وههنا عند التسمية، وذلك يُحَقِّقُهُ لو لم يكن لهما اسم عُرِفا به، ووصف وُصفا به. فإذا كان الله سبحانه فيما اعتقدنا وحدانيته اعتقدنا غير شبيه بالمعروفين في تسمية الآحاد لم يلزمنا التسمية بما تعرفنا ما لولا الاسم لم يجب التشبيه بالاسم لذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن من نفى الأسماء والصفات من الفلسفة لم يقل بالتعطيل، وكل مثبت معناه في التحقيق نفى التعطيل، ثم لم يجب به التشابه، فمثله في الأسماء، وإذا لم يحققوا فما يقولون لو قيل لهم: ما تعبدون، وإلى ماذا تدعون، وبأي دين تدينون، ومن أمركم ونهاكم عما تنهون وتؤمرون، ومن به بدء العالم العلوي والسفلى، وبمن كان أولية الأشياء؟ ليرجعوا إلى معنى يقرب إلى الفهم أو يلحقوا بمنكري حدث العالم. ويبطلوا قولهم في الأول: إنه العقل أو الأصل السابق أو الروحاني الأول، أو ما قالوا في ذلك، وفي ذلك اختيار الحيرة والتمسك بالجهل ودفع ما يعرف غير العالم به، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نذكر طرفا من الشبه التي اعترض من استهواه الشيطان وصرفه بها عما ظهر من البيان ليعلم أن الذي بعثه على ما اختار خدعة نفسه بتسويل عدوه، وذلك لا بتقصير من الله في نصب البرهان، ولا قوة إلا بالله
While the similarity that our imaginations estimate is not from the utterance of the tongue we estimate it, but rather by what we know of the likeness between the two essences and the two actions, so to that it returns, and here at the time of naming, and that verifies it: if there had not been for them a name by which they are known and a description by which they are described. So when Allah, glorified be He, in what we believe of His Oneness, we believe Him not similar to the known ones in the naming of individuals, the naming by what we know does not necessitate us, because without the name the similarity by the name would not be necessary for that reason, and there is no power except by Allah. Moreover, whoever among the philosophers denied names and attributes did not say by nullification (ta'til), and every affirmer's meaning in verification is a denial of nullification, then similarity does not become necessary by it, so it is the same regarding names. And if they do not verify, then what would they say if it were said to them: What do you worship, and to what do you call, and by what religion do you practice, and who commanded you and forbade you from what you forbid and command, and by whom was the beginning of the higher and lower world, and by whom was the priority of things? They would return to a meaning close to understanding or join the deniers of the occurrence of the world. And they would invalidate their saying about the First: that it is the intellect or the prior principle or the first spiritual, or what they said about that, and in that is choosing confusion and clinging to ignorance and repelling what is known by other than the world by it, and there is no power except by Allah. Then we will mention a portion of the doubts that occurred to one whom Satan seduced and diverted him by them from what appeared of the clear proof, so that it may be known that what urged him to what he chose is the deception of his soul by the embellishment of his enemy, and that is not due to any shortcoming from Allah in setting up the proof, and there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on names and attributes, clarifying that similarity is not determined by verbal labels but by actual likeness between essences and actions. Using the same name does not imply similarity; rather, we affirm names for God while negating any literal resemblance. The author then refutes the philosophers who deny names and attributes, noting that they do not actually practice ta'til (complete negation) but affirm some first principle. However, their position leads to confusion and ignorance. The author challenges them: if they deny all attributes, what do they worship? They would have to either return to intelligible meanings or join the deniers of the world's creation. Their characterization of the First as 'intellect' or 'prior principle' is invalid. Finally, the author announces that he will discuss some doubts instigated by Satan to show that those who deviate are deceived, not because of any deficiency in divine proof.

Key Terms
تشابه
ذات
فعل
اسم
وصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the same name for God and creation implies they are similar.
Correction: Similarity is based on essence and action, not the name itself. God's essence is unique, so naming Him does not create likeness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of true similarity?
A) Sharing the same name
B) Likeness between essences and actions
C) Verbal agreement
D) Imaginary estimation

Key Takeaway
Names do not create similarity; similarity is determined by the nature of the essences and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the name 'Creator' for Allah, do you ever subconsciously compare His creative power to human creativity? How does this lesson expose that subtle error, and what can you do to guard your heart against it?
?
Think of a time you described Allah with a name shared by humans (e.g., 'Merciful'). Did that ever make you feel a sense of closeness-by-similarity? According to this passage, where does true similarity lie? How will you adjust your understanding going forward?
?
If someone were to argue, 'Since we call God 'Knowing' and we also say humans are 'knowing', then God must be similar to us in knowledge,' how would you refute them using the exact reasoning in this lesson? More importantly, have you ever made such an assumption in your own heart? Repent and resolve.

Refuting Philosophers Who Deny Divine Names and Attributes
Refuting philosophers who deny names and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's refutation of philosophers who deny names and attributes, including the challenge that their position leads to confusion about worship and their invalid characterization of the First Principle.

Core Idea
Denying divine names leads to ignorance of what to worship.

Imam Maturidi now turns to the philosophers who deny names and attributes. He notes that they do not actually practice complete negation (ta'til), but their position is incoherent. He challenges them: if you deny all names, what do you worship? Their description of the First as 'intellect' or 'spiritual principle' is baseless and leads to confusion. Finally, he announces that he will discuss the doubts that lead people astray.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the refutation.
Note the rhetorical questions posed to the deniers.
2
Understand the critique of philosophers
The author points out that these philosophers do not fully negate; they affirm a first principle. This is inconsistent.
The phrase 'لم يقل بالتعطيل' (did not say by nullification) is key.
3
Analyze the challenge questions
The series of questions ('ما تعبدون' etc.) are designed to expose the incoherence of denying all names.
These questions force the denier to either return to intelligible meaning or join deniers of creation.
4
Examine the invalid characterizations
The author rejects describing the First as 'intellect' or 'prior principle', calling it confusion and ignorance.
This shows that the deniers' alternative is not valid.
5
Note the announcement about doubts
The author says he will mention some doubts instigated by Satan, emphasizing that deviation is due to self-deception, not lack of divine proof.
This sets up the next discussion.

Refutation of Infinite Regress in the Eternity of the World
Maturidi refutes two infinite regress arguments for the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two infinite regress arguments for the world's eternity and how Maturidi refutes them by highlighting logical contradictions.

Core Idea
Infinite regress without a creator or with an eternal cause both lead to rational absurdities.

In this passage, Maturidi begins by addressing those who claim the world is eternal through an infinite chain of causes without a creator. He then turns to a second group who accept a wise originator but still posit an infinite regress. Both views, he argues, suffer from internal inconsistencies that violate the principles of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the first paragraph of the source text and its English translation to get an overview of the two views being refuted.
Pay attention to the terms 'منشئ' (originator) and 'علة' (cause).
2
Identify the first view
The first view claims something comes from something without end and without an originator. Notice why Maturidi says this leads to a negation of reason.
3
Identify the second view
The second view posits an infinite chain with a wise originator as the cause. Note the objection that a cause without an effect is impossible and that it implies either incapacity or necessary actualization.
This argument uses the principle that a cause must have its effect in eternity.
4
Reflect on the logical tools
Consider how Maturidi uses concepts like tasawwur (conception), wahm (estimation), and taqdīr (determination) to critique these views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور: نحن نذكر أقاويل من يدعي قدم العالم، على ما عليه من كون شيء من شيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بلا منشئ، بما كذلك شهده دليل الغائب فيلزم ذلك في الذي غاب؛ لأنه لو جاز إيجاب خلاف [؟] بخلاف المعقول، على أن فيه إيجاب الخروج من التصور في الوهم والتقدير في العقل، وذلك آية نفي العقل، فمثله اعتقاد شيء لا من شيء نحو الأوقات، إنها تنفع [؟] تباعا، وقد اعتبرها بما لا وقت يتوهم إلا وأمكن توهم ذلك قبله إلى ما لا نهاية له، واعتبر أيضا بجواز البقاء بما لا يبقى. ومنهم من يقول يكون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بمنشئ حكيم، وجعلوه علة كون العالم، ومحال كون العلة ولا معلول، مع ما لا يخلو من لا يوصف بالقدرة والجود في القدم، وذلك آية العجز والحاجة، أو يوصف فيجب المقدور عليه وإفاضة الجود على كل شيء، وما ذكر من [؟] التوهم لهم أيضا.

ومنهم من يقول بقدم الطينة وهي الأصل وحدث الصنعة. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: فقوله بقدم الطينة لما ذكرنا من رفع كون شيء لا عن شيء، ثم كان كل شيء حدث عن شيء عند انقلاب الأول وهلاكه نحو ما يحدث من النطفة والبيضة. ومنهم من جعل حدثه بعوارض حلت بالطينة فانقلبت على ما عليه الطبائع من الاعتدال والاختلاف. ومنهم من جعل حدثه بالباري. ومنهم من قال بالأصل وسماه هيولى.

قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: فجملة ما ذهب إليه هؤلاء دفع ما لا [؟].
Abū Manṣūr said: We mention the sayings of those who claim the eternity of the world, according to [the view] of something coming from something without end, without an originator, as such the evidence of the unseen witnesses it, so it is necessary in that which is absent; because if it were permissible to affirm a contrary [؟] contrary to reason, that would involve affirming exit from conception (taṣawwur) in estimation (wahm) and determination (taqdīr) in intellect, and that is a sign of negation of reason. Likewise is believing in something from nothing, like times, they benefit [؟] successively, and he considered them based on [the idea that] there is no time imagined except that its being imagined before it is possible, ad infinitum, and he also considered the permissibility of permanence in that which does not [remain]. And among them are those who say something comes from something without end by a wise originator, and they made it the cause of the world's existence, and it is impossible for the cause to exist without the effect, along with that which is not free from being described with power and generosity in eternity, and that is a sign of incapacity and need; or if described, then what is capable and the outpouring of generosity on everything becomes necessary. And what is mentioned of [؟] their estimation also.

And among them are those who say the clay (ṭīna) is eternal, and it is the origin, and the craftsmanship (ṣanʿa) is originated. Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: His saying about the eternity of the clay [is contradicted] by what we mentioned of removing something coming from nothing, then everything originated came from something upon the transformation and destruction of the first, like what originates from the sperm and the egg. And among them are those who attributed its origination to accidents that occurred to the clay, so it transformed according to the natures of moderation and difference. And among them are those who attributed its origination to the Creator (al-Bārī). And among them are those who said [it is] the origin and called it hyle (hayūlā).

Shaykh Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: In summary, what these people have gone to is repelling what [؟].
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage enumerates and critiques several arguments for the eternity of the world. First, the view that something comes from something in an infinite chain without a creator is refuted by arguing that it leads to logical absurdities contrary to reason. Second, the view that an infinite chain occurs by a wise originator is critiqued because it implies a cause without effect, which is impossible, and also implies either that the cause lacks power and generosity (impossible) or that it must actualize all possibilities. Third, the view that the 'clay' (prime matter) is eternal and the craftsmanship is originated is refuted by noting that any originated thing comes from a prior thing through transformation, thus requiring a creator. Fourth, some attribute the world's origination to accidents in the clay. Fifth, some attribute it to the Creator Himself. Sixth, some call the eternal origin 'hyle' (matter). The author concludes that all these attempts ultimately fail to establish eternity and are refutations of wrong positions.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
منشئ
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress of causes is logically possible without a creator.
Correction: Maturidi shows that infinite regress without an originator contradicts reason because it implies belief in something from nothing and negates rational conception.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what problem arises if a wise originator exists but the world is eternal through an infinite chain?
A) The cause exists without its effect
B) The effect exists without a cause
C) The originator must be described as incapable and needy
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes infinite regress arguments by showing they either negate reason or imply a cause without an effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the beginning of the universe, do you ever find yourself comfortable with an endless chain of causes without a first originator? After reading Maturidi's proof, how does your own rational comfort with infinite regress reveal a hidden denial of reason? What will you actively do next time you catch yourself accepting an infinite regress?
?
Maturidi says that a cause without an effect is impossible. In what area of your life are you treating something as a cause—a habit, a belief, a relationship—that has no real effect, yet you keep it in place? How does this passage force you to confront the emptiness of such a cause? Commit to one concrete step to remove that dead cause today.

Refutation of Eternal Prime Matter Theories
Maturidi refutes theories positing an eternal prime matter or clay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various theories that posit an eternal prime matter (clay or hyle) and how Maturidi refutes them by showing that all originated things require a prior cause.

Core Idea
Claiming that matter is eternal while form is created still requires an external cause for change.

Maturidi continues by examining views that accept an eternal substance (clay) from which the world is formed. Some say the clay is eternal and the craftsmanship is originated; others attribute change to accidents or directly to the Creator. Despite their differences, all fail to escape the need for a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second paragraph and its translation, noting the different positions listed.
2
List the views on eternal matter
Identify the three variants: (a) eternal clay with originated form, (b) change due to accidents, (c) the origin called hyle. Also note the view that attributes origination to the Creator.
3
Understand the refutation
Maturidi's key argument is that everything originated comes from something prior through transformation, so the clay cannot be eternal without a creator. Why does he mention sperm and eggs as examples?
Sperm and eggs are created from prior substances, showing a chain of origination.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the final summary: 'In summary, what these people have gone to is repelling what [?]' – consider that the text may be incomplete, but the idea is that these theories attempt to avoid the truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور: نحن نذكر أقاويل من يدعي قدم العالم، على ما عليه من كون شيء من شيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بلا منشئ، بما كذلك شهده دليل الغائب فيلزم ذلك في الذي غاب؛ لأنه لو جاز إيجاب خلاف [؟] بخلاف المعقول، على أن فيه إيجاب الخروج من التصور في الوهم والتقدير في العقل، وذلك آية نفي العقل، فمثله اعتقاد شيء لا من شيء نحو الأوقات، إنها تنفع [؟] تباعا، وقد اعتبرها بما لا وقت يتوهم إلا وأمكن توهم ذلك قبله إلى ما لا نهاية له، واعتبر أيضا بجواز البقاء بما لا يبقى. ومنهم من يقول يكون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بمنشئ حكيم، وجعلوه علة كون العالم، ومحال كون العلة ولا معلول، مع ما لا يخلو من لا يوصف بالقدرة والجود في القدم، وذلك آية العجز والحاجة، أو يوصف فيجب المقدور عليه وإفاضة الجود على كل شيء، وما ذكر من [؟] التوهم لهم أيضا.

ومنهم من يقول بقدم الطينة وهي الأصل وحدث الصنعة. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: فقوله بقدم الطينة لما ذكرنا من رفع كون شيء لا عن شيء، ثم كان كل شيء حدث عن شيء عند انقلاب الأول وهلاكه نحو ما يحدث من النطفة والبيضة. ومنهم من جعل حدثه بعوارض حلت بالطينة فانقلبت على ما عليه الطبائع من الاعتدال والاختلاف. ومنهم من جعل حدثه بالباري. ومنهم من قال بالأصل وسماه هيولى.

قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: فجملة ما ذهب إليه هؤلاء دفع ما لا [؟].
Abū Manṣūr said: We mention the sayings of those who claim the eternity of the world, according to [the view] of something coming from something without end, without an originator, as such the evidence of the unseen witnesses it, so it is necessary in that which is absent; because if it were permissible to affirm a contrary [؟] contrary to reason, that would involve affirming exit from conception (taṣawwur) in estimation (wahm) and determination (taqdīr) in intellect, and that is a sign of negation of reason. Likewise is believing in something from nothing, like times, they benefit [؟] successively, and he considered them based on [the idea that] there is no time imagined except that its being imagined before it is possible, ad infinitum, and he also considered the permissibility of permanence in that which does not [remain]. And among them are those who say something comes from something without end by a wise originator, and they made it the cause of the world's existence, and it is impossible for the cause to exist without the effect, along with that which is not free from being described with power and generosity in eternity, and that is a sign of incapacity and need; or if described, then what is capable and the outpouring of generosity on everything becomes necessary. And what is mentioned of [؟] their estimation also.

And among them are those who say the clay (ṭīna) is eternal, and it is the origin, and the craftsmanship (ṣanʿa) is originated. Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: His saying about the eternity of the clay [is contradicted] by what we mentioned of removing something coming from nothing, then everything originated came from something upon the transformation and destruction of the first, like what originates from the sperm and the egg. And among them are those who attributed its origination to accidents that occurred to the clay, so it transformed according to the natures of moderation and difference. And among them are those who attributed its origination to the Creator (al-Bārī). And among them are those who said [it is] the origin and called it hyle (hayūlā).

Shaykh Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: In summary, what these people have gone to is repelling what [؟].
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage enumerates and critiques several arguments for the eternity of the world. First, the view that something comes from something in an infinite chain without a creator is refuted by arguing that it leads to logical absurdities contrary to reason. Second, the view that an infinite chain occurs by a wise originator is critiqued because it implies a cause without effect, which is impossible, and also implies either that the cause lacks power and generosity (impossible) or that it must actualize all possibilities. Third, the view that the 'clay' (prime matter) is eternal and the craftsmanship is originated is refuted by noting that any originated thing comes from a prior thing through transformation, thus requiring a creator. Fourth, some attribute the world's origination to accidents in the clay. Fifth, some attribute it to the Creator Himself. Sixth, some call the eternal origin 'hyle' (matter). The author concludes that all these attempts ultimately fail to establish eternity and are refutations of wrong positions.

Key Terms
طينية
صنعة
هيولى
قدم العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter can be eternal and only forms are created, requiring no external cause.
Correction: Maturidi argues that any originated thing (including forms) comes from a prior thing through transformation, so eternal matter still needs a cause to initiate change.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the view that the clay is eternal and the craftsmanship is originated?
A) By affirming that everything comes from nothing
B) By showing that originated things come from prior things through transformation
C) By accepting the eternity of clay
D) By denying the existence of clay

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation of eternal matter theories rests on the principle that all originated entities require a prior cause, thus an eternal substrate cannot explain origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the origins of the universe, do you find any part of your heart leaning towards the idea that matter is eternal, thereby diminishing the absolute need for Allah? What would it take for you to fully embrace the Islamic teaching of creation ex nihilo?
?
Maturidi shows that even acknowledging Allah as the Creator while still positing an eternal substrate falls short. In your daily life, are there areas where you affirm Allah's role but functionally rely on 'eternal' habits or systems as if they are independent? How can you transform that reliance?
?
The principle that every change requires a prior cause points directly to Allah as the First Cause. Does your life reflect that belief, or do you sometimes act as if events happen by chance? What one change will you make today to live with greater awareness of Allah's constant causation?

Refutation of Claiming to Imagine the Unimaginable
Maturidi refutes the opponent's claim to imagine the unimaginable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that imagination cannot be used as proof for the possibility or impossibility of things that are not representable in the mind.

Core Idea
Imagination has limits; what is unimaginable cannot be used as proof.

Maturidi here confronts an opponent who claims that they can imagine something contrary to how it actually is—even that which cannot be represented in the mind. But is imagination really a valid proof? In this passage, Maturidi challenges the opponent's consistency by testing the limits of what can truly be imagined. Read carefully to see how he dismantles the assumption that imagination covers all possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the logical flow: a claim, a challenge, two possible responses, and counterexamples.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and Maturidi's challenge
Find where Maturidi asks the opponent a direct question about imagining the repelling of what is not representable. Then see the two possible answers.
3
Analyze the two responses
For each answer (yes/no), Maturidi gives a consequence. Understand why saying 'yes' is obstinate and why saying 'no' leads to further problems.
Focus on the reasoning, not just the outcome.
4
Consider the counterexamples
Maturidi gives examples of things that are in the mind but not representable, like hearing and sight. Reflect on why these challenge the opponent's claim.
These examples show the limits of imagination.
5
Review the misconception
Read the 'common misunderstanding' and the 'misconception' section to see what wrong belief this passage guards against. Then test yourself with the checkpoint.
This solidifies the lesson's takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتصور في الوهم، ولا يتمثل في النفس؛ إذ كذلك وجد، لم يحتمل قلوبهم إيجاب خلافه فيقال: أيتصور في أوهامكم دفع ما لا يتمثل في النفس؟ فإن قال: نعم، كابر؛ لمشاركتنا إياه في ذي الصور، وليس يتصور دفعه هذا في أوهامنا وإن قال: لا، بل تقديره، فيقال له: متى يتصور في الوهم قدم الشيء أو بقاؤه بعد التفرق، وأن يصير بحيث لا يأخذه البصر. وقد يقول بذلك كله، ذلك في الأنفس ما لا يتمثل في الأنفس من السمع والبصر وجرى قوى جوهر واحد من الطعام وتولد قوة الجواهر المختلفة به كالسمع والبصر والفهم واليد والرجل وغيرها مما يذكر مثله في تلك الجملة بالأدلة.
It is imagined in the imagination, but not represented in the mind; since it is found thus, their hearts do not bear affirming its contrary. So it is said: Do you imagine in your imaginations the repelling of that which is not represented in the mind? If he says: Yes, he is obstinate, due to our sharing with him in the possessor of forms, and its repelling is not imagined in our imaginations. And if he says: No, but rather its estimation, then it is said to him: When is a thing's permanence or its remaining after disintegration imagined in the imagination, and that it becomes such that sight does not grasp it? And he might say all that; that is in the minds what is not represented in the minds, like hearing and sight and the flow of the powers of one substance from food and the generation of the power of different substances by it, like hearing, sight, understanding, hand, foot, and other things similar to that which is mentioned in that summary by proofs.
Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent who claims that they can imagine something contrary to how it actually is. Maturidi first states that there are things imagined in the imagination but not represented in the mind. He then confronts the opponent by asking if they can imagine repelling something that is not representable in the mind. If they say yes, they are obstinate because they share with Maturidi in the realm of forms and yet cannot truly imagine such a repulsion. If they say no, but they still have a 'estimation' (تقدير), Maturidi challenges them to imagine something like the permanence of a thing after its total disintegration, or something becoming too subtle for sight. The opponent might claim to imagine these, but Maturidi points out that there are things in the mind that are not representable, such as the essences of hearing and sight, and how one substance from food generates different powers like hearing, sight, understanding, etc. This shows that the opponent's claim to imagine the unimaginable is inconsistent and that such matters are known through proofs, not raw imagination.

Key Terms
توهم (imagination)
تمثل (representation)
وهم (imagination/mind)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imagination is a valid proof for the possibility or impossibility of something's existence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that imagination has limits; what is unimaginable cannot be used as proof because there are things in the mind that are not representable.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue about the opponent's claim that they can imagine the unimaginable?
A) The opponent is correct because imagination covers all possibilities.
B) The opponent is inconsistent because there are things in the mind that are not representable, yet the opponent claims to imagine them.
C) The opponent is right because the mind can represent everything.
D) Maturidi agrees with the opponent and provides further examples.

Key Takeaway
Imagination cannot be used as a proof for the possibility of what is not representable in the mind, as the mind contains things known through proofs that are not imaginable in a representational sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a belief about Allah or the unseen simply because you could picture it in your mind, even though your heart does not truly affirm it as represented truth? What is that belief, and will you submit it to the proofs that Maturidi speaks of?
?
When you face a reality that is hard to imagine, like the resurrection after disintegration, do you accept it by faith or do you try to force it into your imagination? How can you train yourself to trust what is known through proof over what is merely imaginable?
?
Maturidi points out that even our own faculties like hearing and sight are not representable but are known through evidence. Is your reliance on sensory imagination limiting your spiritual understanding? What step will you take today to prioritize intellectual and revealed proof over imagination?

The Impossibility of Temporal Events in Eternity
Maturidi establishes that temporal events cannot be eternal, using an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal events cannot be attributed to an eternal being, based on Maturidi's argument from the impossibility of imagining timeless change.

Core Idea
Whatever changes cannot be eternal; eternity excludes temporality.

Maturidi begins his refutation of dualism with a general logical principle: that temporal events cannot be associated with eternity. He uses the simple example of an old man's aging to illustrate that describing an eternal being with temporal attributes leads to contradiction. This lesson lays the foundational axiom for the subsequent critique of Manichaean cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial argument
Read the Arabic text carefully; note how Maturidi uses the example of an old man's aging with his feet as a way to question temporal attribution to eternity.
The phrase 'بقدميه' likely means 'with his feet' but the sense is 'at the beginning of his aging'.
2
Identify the key point
The core is: if you consider the idea of a temporal event occurring in eternity impossible, then any temporal event (like movement or rest) cannot be eternal.
This is a logical equivalence.
3
Reflect on the significance
This principle will be used to argue that the mixture of light and darkness cannot be eternal if it is a temporal event.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من التوهم فإنه قد يتوهم في كل شيخ - في أول ما شاخ بقدميه، ولم يجب به الوصف في الأزل به الوصف في الأزل، وكذا في كل حركة وسكون، وتفرق واجتماع. فإن قلت: ذا محال، فمثله كون الحدث في الأزل محال، والله الموفق: ازعم من يقول بالإثنين - الظلة والنور - بقدم العالم، وأحق من يأبى ذلك من بقول بهذا؛ إذ من قولهم إنهما كانا متباينين فامتزجا، فكان العالم من امتزاجها. ومعلوم أن الإمتزاج كان حادثاً، إذ التباين كان هو المتقدم ولم يكونا يلقيان بالعالم. إلا أن يقولوا: النور والظلمة جوهران اختلفا، في الأصل بمكانهما، فكان مكان النوير نور كله وخير، ومكان الظلمة ظلمة كلها وشر، فيبطل القول بقدم العالم الممتزج، وبخاصة قول الماني حيث زعم أن النور لما رأى الظلمة قدحت فيه ومازجت به أحدث هذا العالم؛ ليتخلص بذلك أجزاء النور من أجزاء الظلمه، فصار العالم على هذا القول بعد الامتزاج المحدث مكون بعد المحدث قديما. وذلك [هو] التجاهل. فأوجبوا عجز النور وقت كونه في سلطانه بجميع أعوانه من الخيرات وأنصاره من الحسنات حيث لم يقدر على الإمتناع من قدح الظلمة وأحد احزابه عنه، وجهلوه بوقت القدح فيه ليتخلص منه، ثم وزعموا أنه أحدث هذا العالم ليخلص أجزاءه منه بعد أن صبار في وثاقها - هيهات ما أبعدهم عن ذلك، وما أجهل من يقدمونه ويجعلون له كل وأول كل خير وعلم، وقد جهل ما ذكر، وعظم كل خير بقوة، وقد عجز.
And what was mentioned of misconception—for one might be deluded regarding every old man: at the beginning of his aging with his feet, and the description in eternity is not necessitated by it, similarly in every movement and rest, separation and combination. For if you say: this is impossible, then likewise the occurrence of a temporal event in eternity is impossible. And God is the granter of success: I think that he who says there are two [principles]—darkness and light—[affirms] the pre-eternity of the world, and he who rejects that is more worthy by saying this; for from their sayings is that they were separate, then mixed, so the world came from their mixture. And it is known that the mixture was temporal, since separation was prior, and they were not producing the world. Unless they say: light and darkness are two substances that differed originally in their places, so the place of light was all light and good, and the place of darkness all darkness and evil; then the saying of the pre-eternity of the mixed world is invalidated, especially the saying of Mani where he claimed that when light saw darkness had struck it and mixed with it, it brought about this world to thereby free the parts of light from the parts of darkness. So the world, on this saying, after the temporal mixture, becomes something brought into being after a temporal thing [yet] ancient. And that is ignorance. So they necessitated the incapacity of light at the time of its being in its dominion with all its helpers of good things and supporters of beauties, when it could not prevent the striking of darkness and one of its factions from it, and they made it ignorant of the time of striking into it to free itself. Then they claimed that it brought about this world to free its parts from it after it had become captive in its bonds— far, far are they from that! And how ignorant is he whom they put forward and make him every and the first of every good and knowledge, while he was ignorant of what was mentioned, and magnified every good with power, yet he was powerless.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Manichaean) doctrine that the world originates from the mixture of two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). The passage begins by discussing a misconception (tawahhum) about temporal events and eternity, then transitions to the dualist claim. Maturidi argues that if the two principles were originally separate and then mixed, the mixture itself is a temporal event (hadith), so the world arising from that mixture cannot be eternal. He addresses a possible objection: that light and darkness were always in their respective places but later mixed. This, he says, invalidates the claim of an eternal mixed world. He then critiques Mani's specific claim that light, upon being attacked by darkness, created the world to free its parts. Maturidi points out the logical absurdities: it implies that light was powerless when in its own dominion, unable to prevent the attack, and ignorant of the timing; moreover, it suggests light needed to create a world to liberate itself, but then the world would be contingent on a temporal event, undermining its eternity. The passage ends with a rhetorical condemnation of such ignorance.

Key Terms
التوهم
الحدث
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that temporal changes could be part of an eternal being's nature.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such an idea is a misconception; temporal events are by definition not eternal.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use as an example to illustrate the impossibility of temporal events in eternity?
A) A moving river
B) An old man's aging
C) Light and darkness
D) A stone falling

Key Takeaway
Temporal events cannot be eternal; this is the foundational axiom for refuting dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is eternal and unchanging, what does that mean for your own changing moods and desires? Are you seeking permanence in temporary things, and how can you reorient your heart toward the Eternal?
?
This lesson uses an old man's aging to show that temporal processes cannot be eternal. In your life, what 'aging' (decay, loss, change) do you resist accepting as temporal? How does recognizing its temporality free you from clinging to it?
?
The axiom that temporal events cannot be eternal is a logical principle. How often do you assume that your current difficulties or joys will last forever? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself that only Allah is eternal?
